<div id="review_1277268673" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1277268673" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1277268673?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1277268673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3695338036107218481">Loosely based on the experiences of real-life anthropologists Margaret Mead, Reo Fortune, and Gregory Bateson, <b>Euphoria</b> is a captivating work of historical fiction. Set in the 1930’s Territory of New Guinea, the setting is exotic and the various cultures in the region are intriguing. I became immediately interested in learning about the tribes living along the Sepik River right along with the fictionalized characters Nell Stone and her husband Fen, and the depressed and isolated Andrew Bankson. </span>
  <span id="freeText3695338036107218481" style="display:none">Loosely based on the experiences of real-life anthropologists Margaret Mead, Reo Fortune, and Gregory Bateson, <b>Euphoria</b> is a captivating work of historical fiction. Set in the 1930’s Territory of New Guinea, the setting is exotic and the various cultures in the region are intriguing. I became immediately interested in learning about the tribes living along the Sepik River right along with the fictionalized characters Nell Stone and her husband Fen, and the depressed and isolated Andrew Bankson. These three are drawn to one another; and their interactions, both professionally and romantically, are well developed and quite irresistible. I admired Nell and could feel such empathy for her at times as she struggled not just to understand the people of New Guinea, but humanity itself. <i>“I think above all else it is freedom I search for in my work, in these far-flung places, to find a group of people who give each other the room to be in whatever way they need to be. And maybe I will never find it all in one culture but maybe I find parts of it in several cultures, maybe I can piece it together like a mosaic and unveil it to the world.”</i> The novel is told from Bankson’s point of view, which I found very appealing as his character was more approachable to me and lent an air of nostalgia to the story. Snippets of Nell Stone’s diary entries are also interspersed throughout and add additional perspective to the narrative. <br /><br />Jealousy festers and eventually erupts, violence ensues, and the safety of the threesome becomes threatened and their work halted. I can’t help but think that while we try to penetrate the mysteries of those persons and customs unknown to us, we don’t always understand those that are closest to us either. <i>“I've always been able to see the savageness beneath the veneer of society. It's not so very far beneath the surface, no matter where you go.”</i> This as well as many other penetrating insights provided for a very thought-provoking read. Beautiful prose, tension, haunting atmosphere, and poignant conclusion, make this book stand out in my mind and I believe it will do so for some time to come. My only complaint is that the ending seemed to arrive a bit too quickly. I highly recommend this book. 4.5 stars which I have rounded up to 5 stars. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3695338036107218481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1393769858" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1393769858" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1393769858?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1393769858" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6134341393153224231">Rating: 4* of five<br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: English anthropologist Andrew Bankson has been alone in the field for several years, studying the Kiona river tribe in the Territory of New Guinea. Haunted by the memory of his brothers’ deaths and increasingly frustrated and isolated by his research, Bankson is on the verge of suicide when a chance encounter with colleagues, the controversial Nell Stone and her wry and mercurial Australian husband Fen, pulls him back from the brink. Nell and Fen have just f</span>
  <span id="freeText6134341393153224231" style="display:none">Rating: 4* of five<br /><br /><b>The Publisher Says</b>: English anthropologist Andrew Bankson has been alone in the field for several years, studying the Kiona river tribe in the Territory of New Guinea. Haunted by the memory of his brothers’ deaths and increasingly frustrated and isolated by his research, Bankson is on the verge of suicide when a chance encounter with colleagues, the controversial Nell Stone and her wry and mercurial Australian husband Fen, pulls him back from the brink. Nell and Fen have just fled the bloodthirsty Mumbanyo and, in spite of Nell’s poor health, are hungry for a new discovery. When Bankson finds them a new tribe nearby, the artistic, female-dominated Tam, he ignites an intellectual and romantic firestorm between the three of them that burns out of anyone’s control.<br /><br /><b>My Review</b>: Five stars were well within reach, in fact were more or less guaranteed, but there was a problem. Well, isn't there always. But this is my happy place:<br /><blockquote>I asked her if she believed you could ever truly understand another culture. I told her the longer I stayed, the more asinine the attempt seemed, and that what I’d become more interested in is how we believed we could be objective in any way at all, we who each came in with our own personal definitions of kindness, strength, masculinity, femininity, God, civilisation, right and wrong.</blockquote><br />Yum. And many more like it:<br /><blockquote>It’s that moment about two months in, when you think you’ve finally got a handle on the place. Suddenly it feels within your grasp. It’s a delusion – you’ve only been there eight weeks – and it’s followed by the complete despair of ever understanding anything. But at the moment the place feels entirely yours. It’s the briefest, purest euphoria.</blockquote><br />But the beautiful writing is only part of the story. The plot follows, not overly closely to be sure, the New Guinea experiences of Margaret Mead and her team. But as we draw closer and closer to the end, the setting changes to Australia and becomes pot-boilery, overheated, and unconvincing to me.<br /><br />It is, however, one of the last passages set in the 1930s that made me shout at the page: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The woman DIES HAVING A MISCARRIAGE on the ship back to New York City, and her Australian husband vanishes forver. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Unworthy of a writer of the caliber Lily King is.<br /><br />But the ride...the pages flying and the telephone ignored and the dinner gulped...that can't be discounted or devalued by a misstep, no matter how infuriating I found it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6134341393153224231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10017295673524924431">I loved this quiet, fascinating book about a love triangle between 3 anthropologists in New Guinea in the 1930’s. Lily King poignantly captures a moment in history where tribes and their cultures are still undiscovered, letters and journals are still handwritten and time flows thick as molasses. A deep breath inhaled and held, just before the world changes forever.<br/><br/>Lovely writing and a perfect, haunting ending.</span>

<div id="review_980628952" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Bonnie Brody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7630898-bonnie-brody"><img alt="Bonnie Brody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571268702p2/7630898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/980628952?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bonnie Brody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bonnie Brody" href="/user/show/7630898-bonnie-brody">Bonnie Brody</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer980628952" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9485785512187250444">I had trouble getting through this book. I found the story rather boring and the characters only partially developed. The historic significance of cultural anthropology in New Guinea during the 1930's was very interesting as was the protagonist's character being based on Margaret Mead. This created the groundwork for an interesting historical fiction. <br/><br/>Ms. King appears to have difficulty differentiating between an ethnographic field study as opposed to a treatise on a particular tribe. Ethnograph</span>
  <span id="freeText9485785512187250444" style="display:none">I had trouble getting through this book. I found the story rather boring and the characters only partially developed. The historic significance of cultural anthropology in New Guinea during the 1930's was very interesting as was the protagonist's character being based on Margaret Mead. This created the groundwork for an interesting historical fiction. <br /><br />Ms. King appears to have difficulty differentiating between an ethnographic field study as opposed to a treatise on a particular tribe. Ethnographers are silent observers who enter a domain and try to learn about it from the inside out. This is what the two main characters hoped to attain with their study of the Tam. However, what they achieved was closer to a treatise.<br /><br />Nel Stone and her husband, Fen, have been married for about three years when they meet another anthropologist in New Guinea - Bankson. Bankson is recuperating from a failed suicide attempt and does his best to help out Nell and Fen, who are determined to leave New Guinea unless they can come up with the right type of tribe to study. Bankson is studying the Kiona and he sets Fen and Nell up with with the Tam.<br /><br />This threesome has a difficult dynamic. It is obvious from the outset that Bankson is smitten with Nell despite her having malaria, a broken ankle and sores all over her body. Fen does not show the least bit of jealousy and it is interesting to find out what occurs between Nel and Bankson.<br /><br />I enjoyed the parts about the indigenous people and their lifestyle. The rules and taboos fascinated me and Ms. King successfully communicates this aspect of the novel. However, each of the protagonists lacked a deep sense of inner self and seemed derivatove</span>
  <a data-text-id="9485785512187250444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1223089626" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1223089626" />
    <a title="Caroline " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3236194-caroline"><img alt="Caroline " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576541068p2/3236194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1223089626?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline " href="/user/show/3236194-caroline">Caroline </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802123701" href="/book/show/23168325-euphoria">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=she-wrote-it">she-wrote-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=duped-by-the-blurb">duped-by-the-blurb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=waste-of-trees">waste-of-trees</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=so-much-potential">so-much-potential</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=ho-hum">ho-hum</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1223089626" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16268184818206075419">***NO SPOILERS***<br/><br/>I hate books that waste their potential. The premise of <i>Euphoria</i> is excellent and could've been unlike anything else, but every single one of its plot points is underdeveloped. It paints an incomplete portrait of tribes in New Guinea and of an anthropologist’s fieldwork. The “passionate love triangle” promised in the summary is quite unpassionate and more of a side plot. This love triangle also doesn’t threaten the three main characters’ lives, careers, and bonds, as the summary</span>
  <span id="freeText16268184818206075419" style="display:none">***NO SPOILERS***<br /><br />I hate books that waste their potential. The premise of <i>Euphoria</i> is excellent and could've been unlike anything else, but every single one of its plot points is underdeveloped. It paints an incomplete portrait of tribes in New Guinea and of an anthropologist’s fieldwork. The “passionate love triangle” promised in the summary is quite unpassionate and more of a side plot. This love triangle also doesn’t threaten the three main characters’ lives, careers, and bonds, as the summary states. Here’s a perfect example of a book that’s won a handful of prizes but isn’t necessarily deserving of all the accolades.<br /><br />A book about anthropologists in the 1930s ought to transport and educate, but <i>Euphoria</i> does neither very well. Too often I was <i>told</i> what’s happening in the tribes, not placed in the center of any action. <br /><br />One prime example is when a character recklessly sets off on a life-endangering mission and returns with tragic news. I wasn’t brought along on that mission. I simply saw this character leave in a boat, then return. <br /><br />Herein lies one of <i>Euphoria</i>’s biggest flaws: It lacks drama. There’s no suspense and little tension. It’s a passively told story in which the three anthropologists, Nell (loosely based on Margaret Mead), her husband Fen, and their friend Bankson <i>reported</i> to me. The tribal scenes that <i>are</i> depicted lack the kind of color and energy that would make this story powerful on a deeply emotional level. There’s a remove, the feeling of impassively observing, whether the scene shows Nell sitting cross-legged in her house interacting with the natives or a woman struggling to give birth on a beach. <br /><br />Then there's a love triangle. I'm not automatically opposed to love triangles like some readers are. Generally, I'm opposed to love triangles that don't make sense for the story. With <i>Euphoria</i>, although I'm not sure the love triangle was necessary, it doesn't feel shoehorned in the way triangles in other stories are. However, its portrayal is just not very good. Tension is weak, only simmering under the surface, never reaching a boiling point of intensity. <br /><br />This isn't helped by the fact that this triangle involves three characters King failed to make authentically human. Probably because this story was inspired by the life of Margaret Mead, King devoted most of her attention to Nell. Nell is therefore fleshed out pretty well; however, King gave surprisingly short shrift to Nell’s husband Fen. He’s a cardboard character who’s never not an uncaring husband and often an uninterested anthropologist who increasingly annoys Nell. When he gets jealous, viciousness rises to the surface in a flash. <br /><br />Nell’s lover, Bankson, by comparison, is sensitive, gentle, lonely, and as passionate about anthropology as Nell is. It’s impossible not to feel sorry for him when he expresses how lonely he often feels and to not adore how he treats Nell. <br /><br />It seems time and again, when a writer crafts a love triangle involving married people, portrayals of the cheated-on spouse and the lover end up black-and-white. To make an extramarital affair palatable, it’s essential that the cheated-on spouse be a one-dimensional jerk and the lover close to perfect. <br /><br /><i>Euphoria</i> does have great writing going for it, which is where some of the accolades no doubt come from. King’s writing style is outstanding, spare but not simplistic, sophisticated but not at all flowery. King also did a good job making me feel Bankson's loneliness and hopelessness:<blockquote>Nell and Fen had chased away my thoughts of suicide. But what had they left me with? Fierce desires, a great tide of feeling of which I could make little sense, an ache that seemed to have no name but want. I want. Intransitive. No object. It was the opposite of wanting to die. But it was scarcely more bearable.</blockquote>Pacing, too, is even, albeit slow because of the lack of drama and an underdeveloped love story that gathers speed too late in the story. The ending is, fortunately, satisfying--emotional and memorable. <br /><br />As an anthropological story, <i>Euphoria</i> fails to offer insight into anthropological fieldwork and tribal life. A better choice would be <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/531016.Dancing_Skeletons_Life_and_Death_in_West_Africa" title="Dancing Skeletons Life and Death in West Africa by Katherine A. Dettwyler" rel="nofollow noopener">Dancing Skeletons: Life and Death in West Africa</a>. Those seeking a romance in an exotic setting also should opt for something else. <i>Euphoria</i> is worth reading only to see what good writing looks like. That's it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16268184818206075419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1130625293" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130625293" />
    <a title="Iris P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p"><img alt="Iris P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483406411p2/2641948.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130625293?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iris P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iris P" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p">Iris P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22032840-euphoria">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=2015-favorites">2015-favorites</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=favorites-all-of-time">favorites-all-of-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=iris-s-favorites">iris-s-favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130625293" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8644216024321889904"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22032840.Euphoria' title='Euphoria by Lily King' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1399056512s/22032840.jpg' alt='Euphoria by Lily King' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22032840.Euphoria' title='Euphoria by Lily King' rel='nofollow noopener'>Euphoria</a><br/><br/>Please be aware that my review contain what could be considered spoilers!!!!<br/><br/>I read somewhere that Euphoria is a novel of ideas. At its center, the novel follows three young anthropologists and the very strange romantic triangle that takes place between them in the early 1930’s during a short period of time when their lives and careers intersected along the Sepik River in New Guinea. <br/>Lily King based the main three characters, Nell Stone, Fenwick Schuyler & Andrew Bankson, on the lives</span>
  <span id="freeText8644216024321889904" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22032840.Euphoria" title="Euphoria by Lily King" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1399056512s/22032840.jpg" alt="Euphoria by Lily King" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22032840.Euphoria" title="Euphoria by Lily King" rel="nofollow noopener">Euphoria</a><br><br>Please be aware that my review contain what could be considered spoilers!!!!<br><br>I read somewhere that Euphoria is a novel of ideas. At its center, the novel follows three young anthropologists and the very strange romantic triangle that takes place between them in the early 1930’s during a short period of time when their lives and careers intersected along the Sepik River in New Guinea. <br>Lily King based the main three characters, Nell Stone, Fenwick Schuyler &amp; Andrew Bankson, on the lives of pioneer anthropologist Margaret Mead and her 2nd and 3rd husbands, Reo Fortune &amp; Gregory Bateson.<br>Nell is an American, who is already well known for having published a rather controversial book called “Children of the Kirakira”, her husband Fen is an Australian, who’s very envious of his wife’s accomplishments, and Bankson is a British anthropologist who has survived a suicide attempt.<br><br>Bankson is the main narrator, but Nell’s diary entries are intertwined as part of his narrative and somehow the author makes this work beautifully. If anything this structure enhances the suspense and sense of foreboding that are present from the beginning of the book.<br><br>I adored the characters of Nell and Bankson. When they met, there’s an immediate connection between them, both romantically and intellectually. They seem to complement and respect each other, something that Fen is unable to provide for Nell, since he feels constantly threatened by his wife’s success.<br><br>There are times when if sounds as if Nell believes that a relationship with Bankson could help balance her broken marriage, although I am not sure that is clear how exactly this was supposed to work. She is also obsessed with the idea of becoming a mother, so this also plays an important part of her psyche and motivations.<br><br>Euphoria gave me the opportunity to learn a little bit about Anthropology at a time when it was an emerging science, so to me the most fascinating part of the book is to be there as new theories and ideas take form in the minds of these young scientists. When it comes to Anthropology, these people were developing theories while observing human behavior, and nothing had been written yet.<br>And what a delight it is to experience the passion and dedication they show while observing these tribes with different traditions and social structures, compare to our Western way of life.<br><br>You should know that this is not a scientific book of course, but science provides the tapestry where the novel develops, and the character’s ambitions and pursuit of fame are intrinsically tied to the story at large and its tragic conclusion.<br><br>Euphoria’s title comes from Nell’s description of “that moment two months in, when you think you’ve finally got a handle on the place,” inevitably “followed by the complete despair of ever understanding anything”. There are a couple of times in the book where Nell, Fen &amp; Bankson, work very well together, they have these brainstorming sessions that feel so intense, it’s almost as if they are in a trance.<br><br>So I love everything about this book, from its cover and title, to the characters, the sensual tension, the feminist undertones and its poignant portrait of a time when scientists were sort of celebrities and were much more part of pop culture. Achieving success as a scientist provided a path to glory, fame and immortality in a way that I believe no longer describes how society looks at scientists today (with a few exceptions of course).<br><br>I should say, that I enjoyed both of the audiobook narrators immensely, their performances certainly helped enhance the story for me.<br><br><a href="http://s1341.photobucket.com/user/irisper01/media/thenewyorker1932cover_zpszd0xiq6j.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424981696i/13826995._SY540_.jpg" alt="Euphoria photo thenewyorker1932cover_zpszd0xiq6j.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>August 20, 1932 New Yorker Cover described by Bankson towards the end of the book<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="8644216024321889904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1329136299" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1329136299" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1166663-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277905205p2/1166663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1329136299?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/1166663-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1329136299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15583961500221382796"><b>The Hook</b> - Just read the glowing reviews and you’ll understand why I had to read <u>Euphoria</u>. My sincere thanks also to our Adult Services Librarian, Cait, at my public library for her endorsement of this book. <br/><br/><b>The Line</b> – <i>“But she was aware the story you think you know is never the real one.”</i><br/><br/><b>The Sinker</b> – I don’t know much about Margaret Mead’s life except that she was a cultural anthropologist. Loosely based on Mead’s life, <u>Euphoria</u> may find me scrambling to learn more. <br/><br/>Anthropology is key here but</span>
  <span id="freeText15583961500221382796" style="display:none"><b>The Hook</b> - Just read the glowing reviews and you’ll understand why I had to read <u>Euphoria</u>. My sincere thanks also to our Adult Services Librarian, Cait, at my public library for her endorsement of this book. <br /><br /><b>The Line</b> – <i>“But she was aware the story you think you know is never the real one.”</i><br /><br /><b>The Sinker</b> – I don’t know much about Margaret Mead’s life except that she was a cultural anthropologist. Loosely based on Mead’s life, <u>Euphoria</u> may find me scrambling to learn more. <br /><br />Anthropology is key here but if that’s not your cup of tea, don’t be swayed from reading this lush novel. <u>Euphoria</u> is set in the early 30’s in New Guinea on the Sepik River and concerns a love triangle or perhaps a quadrangle. <br /><br />It is infrequent that I say less is more, but that’s how I feel about <u>Euphoria</u>. There are many fine other reviews for you to read. Have faith in my rating and add it to your list. <br /><br /><u>Euphoria</u> is heading to the silver screen and will be directed by Michael Apted. I can see it being a hit <br /><br />One last thought. I listened to <u>Euphoria</u> but would suggest you read it if you can. This is not to say that the dual cast recording by Simon Vance and Xe Sands is not exquisite. It is pitch perfect and beautifully paced. AudioFile magazine sums it up this way:<br /><br /> <i> Delivering the alternating chapters of Mead’s stand-in, American Nell Stone, and Englishman Andrew Bankson, Sands and Vance perform the stupendous feat of creating memorable versions of the same characters.”</i>|<br /><br /> If I had it to do over again I’d luxuriate in the rich language, its sensuality as well as its sultry nature. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15583961500221382796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3111298793242453142"><b>"The Mumbanyo kill their twins, she told me close to morning, because two babies meant two different lovers."</b><p>The remote villages of 1930's New Guinea <i>really</i> come to life in this descriptive story of three anthropologists who interview and document their interactions with native tribes as they live in their environment.</p><p>As relationships develop and dubious personalities show their true form, it becomes apparent there is evil in the air and danger on the way.....with one character in particular you</p></span>
  <span id="freeText3111298793242453142" style="display:none"><b>"The Mumbanyo kill their twins, she told me close to morning, because two babies meant two different lovers."</b><p>The remote villages of 1930's New Guinea <i>really</i> come to life in this descriptive story of three anthropologists who interview and document their interactions with native tribes as they live in their environment.</p><p>As relationships develop and dubious personalities show their true form, it becomes apparent there is evil in the air and danger on the way.....with one character in particular you will love to hate!</p><p>Super interesting read! Great bookclub pick!</p><p>

<div id="review_3312941361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3312941361" />
    <a title="Bianca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29843800-bianca"><img alt="Bianca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589154116p2/29843800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3312941361?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bianca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bianca" href="/user/show/29843800-bianca">Bianca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=female-author">female-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=us-author">us-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3312941361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11259626736909230368">After reading Lily King's <i>Writers and Lovers</i> I was keen to explore <i>Euphoria</i>, which had been praised by many GR friends.<br/><br/>It's a small novel that packed a lot. Its setting was original and so was the plot. I don't recall ever reading a book set in Papua New Guinea and certainly not among the primitive tribes strewn about in the 1930s. My knowledge of cultural anthropology was very limited so I've learned something new which is always a bonus.<br/><br/>The main characters - Nell Stone, her husband Fen, and th</span>
  <span id="freeText11259626736909230368" style="display:none">After reading Lily King's <i>Writers and Lovers</i> I was keen to explore <i>Euphoria</i>, which had been praised by many GR friends.<br /><br />It's a small novel that packed a lot. Its setting was original and so was the plot. I don't recall ever reading a book set in Papua New Guinea and certainly not among the primitive tribes strewn about in the 1930s. My knowledge of cultural anthropology was very limited so I've learned something new which is always a bonus.<br /><br />The main characters - Nell Stone, her husband Fen, and the Brit Andrew Bankson, all three anthropologists - were well-drawn and interesting. Obsessive working and ambition, competition, jealousy and resentment among the married couple, power dynamics, ethical issues and methodology were some of the issues to ponder and observe in this novel. The love triangle made things more interesting. <br /><br /><i>Euphoria</i> -(I love the word and the feeling) - is a well-realised literary novel with fantastic writing, excellent characterisations and an interesting plot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11259626736909230368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer982626957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12863972614337785772">It has been a while since I had the urge to literally bring a book to my chest and hold it there for a few moments after finishing it, but with this book I not only had the urge, I embraced it. (And therefore the book, because I am both literal and ridiculous.) <br/><br/>This is one of those rare books I feel compelled to order for my closest friends (Maria, consider yourself warned!) rather than just recommend, because <i>it's just that good.</i> The tone, like the prose itself, is gorgeous and evocative, the s</span>
  <span id="freeText12863972614337785772" style="display:none">It has been a while since I had the urge to literally bring a book to my chest and hold it there for a few moments after finishing it, but with this book I not only had the urge, I embraced it. (And therefore the book, because I am both literal and ridiculous.) <br /><br />This is one of those rare books I feel compelled to order for my closest friends (Maria, consider yourself warned!) rather than just recommend, because <i>it's just that good.</i> The tone, like the prose itself, is gorgeous and evocative, the story and characters powerfully drawn and incredibly nuanced. My only wish is that it was longer. Not because it feels abrupt or un-finished, but because I wanted so much more of and <i>FOR</i> both Nell and Andrew. Both characters immediately resonated with me, and I honestly had a feeling from the very first chapters that this book would end up on my list of favorites. This is a work of great emotional depth and sensitivity, of powerful insight, and sublime beauty. And (I apologize for quoting a phrase that will only make sense to those who have already read it) this book is both <i>"wine and bread and deep in my stomach."</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12863972614337785772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText155277669524729403" style="display:none"><b>4.5★</b><br />I loved this exotic tale of three anthropologists in New Guinea during the 1930s. While they are observing, studying, and disrupting the lives of the tribes they are living amongst, the reader feels like a fourth character watching the dangerous fascinations, jealousy, and erotic tension that escalate between the two men and one woman. The wonderful pacing and prose keeps you reading in your own quest for fulfillment and release. <br /> <br /></span>

<div id="review_925549918" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/925549918" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/925549918?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781443435291" href="/book/show/19367508-euphoria">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=read-via-netgalley">read-via-netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer925549918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11276598428257444837"><b>“The story you think you know is never the real one.”</b> What a great novel about the love triangle between three anthropologists (one American, one English, and one Australian) working among the tribes of Papua New Guinea in the 1930s. Apparently it’s based on the lives of Margaret Mead and two of her husbands, but knowledge of the real-life antecedents is unnecessary; this book is its own intense, self-contained world. Two other novels I’ve read in the past few years, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/683936810' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>The People in the Trees</i>
</a> </span>
  <span id="freeText11276598428257444837" style="display:none"><b>“The story you think you know is never the real one.”</b> What a great novel about the love triangle between three anthropologists (one American, one English, and one Australian) working among the tribes of Papua New Guinea in the 1930s. Apparently it’s based on the lives of Margaret Mead and two of her husbands, but knowledge of the real-life antecedents is unnecessary; this book is its own intense, self-contained world. Two other novels I’ve read in the past few years, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/683936810" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The People in the Trees</i>
</a> by Hanya Yanigahara and <i>State of Wonder</i> by Ann Patchett, have very similar themes of tribal savagery vs. Western ‘civilization’, but this for me was much the most psychologically satisfying adventure tale of the three.<br /><br />The narration struck me as very interesting: you’re convinced it’s from a third-person omniscient perspective until the very last sentence of the first chapter, when you realize it’s actually from Andy Bankson’s first-person point-of-view. Nell’s field journals are then a way of interspersing her own thoughts (though I reckon having excerpts from her published books could have been another strategy for introducing a different voice and register). Bankson (the Englishman) is a melancholy and introspective narrator: “I find I am more and more interested in this question of subjectivity, of the limited lens of the anthropologist, than I am in the traditions and habits of the Kiona. <b>Perhaps all science is merely self-investigation</b>.”<br /><br />Nell (the Margaret Mead character) is wonderfully plucky – observant and forceful, yet lovable for her utter enthusiasm for her work. As her first school report card read, “Elinor has an overenthusiasm for her own ideas and a voluble dearth for those of others.” Despite her distaste for some of the tribal practices she’s come across (infanticide, polyamory), she relishes the task of learning a culture. What she loves most is “that moment about two months in, when you think you’ve finally got a handle on the place. Suddenly it feels within your grasp. It’s a delusion...and it’s followed by the complete despair of ever understanding anything. But at that moment the place feels entirely yours. It’s the briefest, purest <b>euphoria</b>.”<br /><br /><br />(By the by, I’m not sure there has ever been a more excruciating sex scene than the one in the first chapter. This doesn’t bode well: “‘Time to procreate,’ [Fen] said in a singsong.” And on it goes from there. King should definitely have been up for a “<a href="http://www.literaryreview.co.uk/badsexshortlist2014.php" rel="nofollow noopener">Bad Sex</a>” award for this one! [Although the prize’s remit is slightly different.] It’s well written, of course, but cringe-worthy to imagine.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11276598428257444837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_830577170" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/830577170" />
    <a title="Diane S ☔" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s"><img alt="Diane S ☔" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598402138p2/4159922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/830577170?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane S ☔" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane S ☔" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s">Diane S ☔</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer830577170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13530066141672439510">Early 1930's finds Banks on living with the Kiona tribe in New Guineas. Depressed due to his solitude and loneliness, he tries to take his own life, an attempt that fails. Nell Stone and her husband Fen, have just left the tribe, a very violent one, that they have been studying. Bankson finds them a tribe on the Sepik river, an hour away from the Kiona by boat.<br/><br/>This was an extremely interesting read, the tribal customs, their rituals and beliefs, the way they related to each other are all display</span>
  <span id="freeText13530066141672439510" style="display:none">Early 1930's finds Banks on living with the Kiona tribe in New Guineas. Depressed due to his solitude and loneliness, he tries to take his own life, an attempt that fails. Nell Stone and her husband Fen, have just left the tribe, a very violent one, that they have been studying. Bankson finds them a tribe on the Sepik river, an hour away from the Kiona by boat.<br /><br />This was an extremely interesting read, the tribal customs, their rituals and beliefs, the way they related to each other are all displayed in this well researched novel. There is jealousy, Nell has published and her book has created quite a stir in academic circles, a fact Fen resents. There is a betrayal when Fen does something unpardonable and they are forced to flee. There is competition, as all three are ardent anthropologists, and lastly there is greed and a forbidden love. In immersive prose, King has written a wonderful story loosely based on the lives of Margaret Mead and her second and third husbands, all successful in their own right.<br /><br />I have not read many books, if any, on anthropology, so knew little about these people I was reading about. This book fascinated me enough to acquire the book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/111373.Intertwined_Lives_Margaret_Mead__Ruth_Benedict__and_Their_Circle" title="Intertwined Lives Margaret Mead, Ruth Benedict, and Their Circle by Lois W. Banner" rel="nofollow noopener">Intertwined Lives: Margaret Mead, Ruth Benedict, and Their Circle</a>, which I hope to get to soon. My only complaint is that I wish an author's note was included.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13530066141672439510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer959203376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10964763193018337349">Euphoria will definitely be added to my favorites shelf. Truly mesmerizing.<br/> <i>'Do you have a favorite part of all this?' she asked.<br/> 'All what?' I said.<br/> 'This work.'<br/> Favorite part? There was little at this point that<br/> didn't make me want to run with stones straight back<br/> to the river. I shook my head. 'You first.'<br/> She looked surprised, as if she hadn't expected the<br/> question to come back at her. She narrowed her grey<br/> eyes. 'It's that moment about two months in, when<br/> you think you've finally got a hand</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10964763193018337349" style="display:none">Euphoria will definitely be added to my favorites shelf. Truly mesmerizing.<br /> <i>'Do you have a favorite part of all this?' she asked.<br /> 'All what?' I said.<br /> 'This work.'<br /> Favorite part? There was little at this point that<br /> didn't make me want to run with stones straight back<br /> to the river. I shook my head. 'You first.'<br /> She looked surprised, as if she hadn't expected the<br /> question to come back at her. She narrowed her grey<br /> eyes. 'It's that moment about two months in, when<br /> you think you've finally got a handle on the place. <br /> Suddenly it feels within your grasp. It's a delusion<br /> --you've only been here eight weeks--and it's<br /> followed by the complete despair of ever understanding<br /> anything. But at that moment the place feels entirely <br /> yours. It's the briefest, purest euphoria.</i> (loc 635)<br /><br />In this exciting book about people and many types of relationships, we are witness to the journey of three anthropologists in New Guinea in the 1930s, a young married couple, Nell and Fen, who are seeking a new tribe to monitor and Bankston, who has been on his own for years, too alone. The three meet by chance and their lives become entwined, not so much by chance. There are so many layers of behavior in this novel---it is structured beautifully. There are the anthropologists of course, and the various tribes involved. But then there are the interactions amongst the three scientists and all of their motivations and emotions. <br /><br />The narrative voice shifts from Bankston to Nell and back in a way that gives a full picture of most of the physical action. King writes a beautifully controlled story with just the right amount of tension, just the right hints, that kept me plowing on through to see what would happen with the tribes, with the incipient love triangle.<br /><br />Very much recommended. A strong 5*<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="10964763193018337349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Linda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9472704-linda"><img alt="Linda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336837712p2/9472704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1199271147?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda" href="/user/show/9472704-linda">Linda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9472704-linda?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1199271147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4012729683372846570">I wasn't quite sure what I really expected in Euphoria by Lily King. I did know that this novel would be based loosely on life encounters of the anthropologist Margaret Mead.<br/><br/>What actually made my eyelashes flutter was the character of Andrew Bankson. Lily King develops him beautifully throughout the book. It is Bankson who touches the heart and the soul. Bankson and his brothers are the tragic products of life energy spent in pleasing others and in the senseless pursuit of outward approval. Ente</span>
  <span id="freeText4012729683372846570" style="display:none">I wasn't quite sure what I really expected in Euphoria by Lily King. I did know that this novel would be based loosely on life encounters of the anthropologist Margaret Mead.<br /><br />What actually made my eyelashes flutter was the character of Andrew Bankson. Lily King develops him beautifully throughout the book. It is Bankson who touches the heart and the soul. Bankson and his brothers are the tragic products of life energy spent in pleasing others and in the senseless pursuit of outward approval. Enter Nell Stone and her Australian husband Fen. At last, Branson can lift his eyes upward and crawl painsakingly out of his downward spiral. Nell engages him in a true reflection of himself. Does Bankson serve as a genuine relationship for Nell or as simply a diversion from her controlling, over-bearing husband?<br /><br />The setting in New Guinea highlights the work of anthropologists of the time period. The culture, demeanor, and ritualistic practices of the various tribes along the river make and break the lives of Andrew, Nell, and Fen. Being observationalists of the daily lives of these tribes comes with great sacrifice. The physical and emotional toll comes to the forefront.<br /><br />But it is the very personal encounters of Andrew, Nell, and Fen that weave their way throughout this book. That is why Lily King is a master at presenting the inner workings of her characters. Nature has transformed them from their former selves, but human nature leads them in questionable directions. A very good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4012729683372846570" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ya3NHsMlJpCbpUsI6iN8QF0aBjOMt5yJy5t85LyjZqnTN8tfaut16d+c2HR/lydGD7f3xpqnvbZjIpJPI7NTxA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

He is also a Chippewa Council member who is trying to understand the consequences of a new “emancipation” bill on its way to the floor of the United States Congress. It is 1953 and he and the other council members know the bill isn’t about freedom; Congress is fed up with Indians. The bill is a “termination” that threatens the rights of Native Americans to their land and their very identity. How can the government abandon treaties made in good faith with Native Americans “for as long as the grasses shall grow, and the rivers run”?\n\nSince graduating high school, Pixie Paranteau has insisted that everyone call her Patrice. Unlike most of the girls on the reservation, Patrice, the class valedictorian, has no desire to wear herself down with a husband and kids. She makes jewel bearings at the plant, a job that barely pays her enough to support her mother and brother. Patrice’s shameful alcoholic father returns home sporadically to terrorize his wife and children and bully her for money. But Patrice needs every penny to follow her beloved older sister, Vera, who moved to the big city of Minneapolis. Vera may have disappeared; she hasn’t been in touch in months, and is rumored to have had a baby. Determined to find Vera and her child, Patrice makes a fateful trip to Minnesota that introduces her to unexpected forms of exploitation and violence, and endangers her life.\n\nThomas and Patrice live in this impoverished reservation community along with young Chippewa boxer Wood Mountain and his mother Juggie Blue, her niece and Patrice’s best friend Valentine, and Stack Barnes, the white high school math teacher and boxing coach who is hopelessly in love with Patrice.\n\nIn the Night Watchman, Louise Erdrich creates a fictional world populated with memorable characters who are forced to grapple with the worst and best impulses of human nature. Illuminating the loves and lives, the desires and ambitions of these characters with compassion, wit, and intelligence, The Night Watchman is a majestic work of fiction from this revered cultural She yearns for a pursuit worthy of her life, but finds no outlet for her considerable talents. Defying the expectations placed on women, she engages in furtive scholarly pursuits and writes secret narratives about neglected and silenced women. When she meets the eighteen-year-old Jesus, each is drawn to and enriched by the other’s spiritual and philosophical ideas. He becomes a floodgate for her intellect, but also the awakener of her heart.\n\nTheir marriage unfolds with love and conflict, humor and pathos in Nazareth, where Ana makes a home with Jesus, his brothers, James and Simon, and their mother, Mary. Here, Ana’s pent-up longings intensify amid the turbulent resistance to the Roman occupation of Israel, partially led by her charismatic adopted brother, Judas. She is sustained by her indomitable aunt Yaltha, who is searching for her long-lost daughter, as well as by other women, including her friend Tabitha, who is sold into slavery after she was raped, and Phasaelis, the shrewd wife of Herod Antipas. Ana’s impetuous streak occasionally invites danger. When one such foray forces her to flee Nazareth for her safety shortly before Jesus’s public ministry begins, she makes her way with Yaltha to Alexandria, where she eventually finds refuge and purpose in unexpected surroundings.\n\nGrounded in meticulous historical research and written with a reverential approach to Jesus’s life that focuses on his humanity, The Book of Longings is an inspiring But she’s also tough and smart, with an indomitable will, and she is not afraid: not of her cousin’s new husband, who is both menacing and alluring; not of his father, the ancient patriarch who seems to be fascinated by Noemí; and not even of the house itself, which begins to invade Noemi’s dreams with visions of blood and doom. \n\nHer only ally in this inhospitable abode is the family’s youngest son. Shy and gentle, he seems to want to help Noemí but might also be hiding dark knowledge of his family’s past. For there are many secrets behind the walls of High Place. The family’s once colossal wealth and faded mining empire kept them from prying eyes, but as Noemí digs deeper she unearths stories of violence and madness. \n\nAnd Noemí, mesmerized by the terrifying       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/117786.Lily_King">About Lily King</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/117786.Lily_King"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1404743838p3/117786.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/117786.Lily_King">Lily King
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,924
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4127031064582942096"><a href='http://www.lilykingbooks.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lily King</a> grew up in Massachusetts and received her B.A. in English Literature from University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill, and her M.A. in Creative Writing from Syracuse University. She has taught English and Creative Writing at several universities and high schools in this country and abroad. Lily's new novel, <i>Euphoria</i>, was released in June 2014. It has drawn significant acclaim so far, bei</span>
  <span id="freeText4127031064582942096" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.lilykingbooks.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Lily King</a> grew up in Massachusetts and received her B.A. in English Literature from University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill, and her M.A. in Creative Writing from Syracuse University. She has taught English and Creative Writing at several universities and high schools in this country and abroad. Lily's new novel, <i>Euphoria</i>, was released in June 2014. It has drawn significant acclaim so far, being named an Amazon Book of the Month, on the Indie Next List, and hitting numerous summer reading lists from <i>The Boston Globe</i> to <i>O Magazine</i> and <i>USA Today</i>. Reviewed on the cover of <i>The New York Times Book Review</i>, Emily Eakin called <i>Euphoria</i>, “a taut, witty, fiercely intelligent tale of competing egos and desires in a landscape of exotic menace.”<br /><br />Lily’s first novel, <i>The Pleasing Hour</i> (1999) won the Barnes and Noble Discover Award and was a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book and an alternate for the PEN/Hemingway Award. Her second, <i>The English Teacher</i>, was a <i>Publishers Weekly</i> Top Ten Book of the Year, a <i>Chicago Tribune</i> Best Book of the Year, and the winner of the Maine Fiction Award. Her third novel, <i>Father of the Rain</i> (2010), was a <i>New York Times</i> Editors Choice, a <i>Publishers Weekly</i> Best Novel of the Year and winner of both the New England Book Award for Fiction and the Maine Fiction Award. <br /><br />Lily is the recipient of a MacDowell Fellowship and a Whiting Writer's Award. Her short fiction has appeared in literary magazines including Ploughshares and Glimmer Train, as well as in several anthologies.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4127031064582942096" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/26127419-euphoria">Quotes from Euphoria</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You don’t realise how language actually interferes with communication until you don’t have it, how it gets in the way like an overdominant sense.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1268229-you-don-t-realise-how-language-actually-interferes-with-communication-until">44 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You don't realize how language actually interferes with communication until you don't have it, how it gets in the way like an overdominant sense. You have to pay much more attention to everything else when you can't understand the words. Once comprehension comes, so much else falls away. You then rely on their words, and words aren't always the most reliable thing.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/6474323-you-don-t-realize-how-language-actually-interferes-with-communication-until">39 likes</a>
